   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Space-based solar power
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">

     </a>

     </a>

     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Suntower.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1296" data-file-width="2018" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Suntower.jpg/300px-Suntower.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Suntower.jpg/450px-Suntower.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Suntower.jpg/600px-Suntower.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Suntower.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        NASA Suntower concept.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <b>
       Space-based solar power
      </b>
      (
      <b>
       SBSP
      </b>
      ) is the concept of collecting
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">
       solar power
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">
       space
      </a>
      (using an "SPS", that is, a "solar-power satellite" or a "satellite power system") for use on
      <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
       Earth
      </a>
      . It has been in research since the early 1970s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESA-SBSP_1-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ESA-SBSP-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DOE-SBSP_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-DOE-SBSP-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      SBSP would differ from current
      <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
       solar
      </a>
      collection methods in that the means used to collect
      <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">
       energy
      </a>
      would reside on an
      <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">
       orbiting
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">
       satellite
      </a>
      instead of on Earth's surface. Some projected benefits of such a system are a higher collection rate and a longer collection period due to the lack of a diffusing
      <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">
       atmosphere
      </a>
      and night
      <a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">
       time
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">
       space
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Part of the
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">
       solar energy
      </a>
      (55–60%) is lost on its way through the atmosphere by the effects of
      <a href="/wiki/Reflection_(physics)" title="Reflection (physics)">
       reflection
      </a>
      and absorption. Space-based solar power systems convert
      <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">
       sunlight
      </a>
      to microwaves outside the atmosphere, avoiding these losses, and the downtime (and
      <a href="/wiki/Lambert%27s_cosine_law" title="Lambert's cosine law">
       cosine losses
      </a>
      , for fixed flat-plate collectors) due to the
      <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation" title="Earth's rotation">
       Earth's rotation
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Besides the cost of implementing such a system, SBSP also introduces several new hurdles, primarily the problem of transmitting energy from orbit to Earth's surface for use. Since wires extending from Earth's surface to an orbiting satellite are neither practical nor feasible with current technology, SBSP designs generally include the use of some manner of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wireless_power_transmission" title="Wireless power transmission">
       wireless power transmission
      </a>
      . The collecting satellite would convert solar energy into electrical energy on board, powering a
      <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">
       microwave
      </a>
      transmitter or
      <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">
       laser
      </a>
      emitter, and focus its beam toward a collector (
      <a href="/wiki/Rectenna" title="Rectenna">
       rectenna
      </a>
      ) on Earth's surface.
      <a href="/wiki/Radiation_damage" title="Radiation damage">
       Radiation
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Micrometeoroid" title="Micrometeoroid">
       micrometeoroid
      </a>
      damage could also become concerns for SBSP.
     </p>
     <p>
      SBSP is considered a form of
      <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_energy" title="Sustainable energy">
       sustainable or green energy
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">
       renewable energy
      </a>
      , and is occasionally considered among
      <a href="/wiki/Climate_engineering" title="Climate engineering">
       climate engineering
      </a>
      proposals. It is attractive to those seeking large-scale solutions to
      <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">
       anthropogenic climate change
      </a>
      or fossil fuel depletion (such as
      <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">
       peak oil
      </a>
      ).
     </p>
     <p>
      SBSP is being actively pursued by Japan and China. In 2008 Japan passed its Basic Space Law which established Space Solar Power as a national goal
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        [3]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and JAXA has a roadmap to commercial SBSP. In 2015 the China Academy for Space Technology (CAST) briefed their roadmap at the International Space Development Conference (ISDC) where they showcased their road map to a 1 GW commercial system in 2050 and unveiled a
      <a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=XhgJwnpYRGc" rel="nofollow">
       video
      </a>
      and
      <a class="external text" href="http://spacejournal.ohio.edu/issue18/cast.html" rel="nofollow">
       description
      </a>
      of their design. A
      <a class="external text" href="http://d3ssp.org/" rel="nofollow">
       proposal
      </a>
      for the United States to lead in Space Solar Power has recently received high level attention after it won the D3 (Diplomacy, Development, Defense) competition sponsored by the Secretary of Defense, Secretary of State, and USAID Director.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
      As of May 21, 2015, there was an active petition on Change.org and a second active petition at Whitehouse website.
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Space_to_ground_microwave,_laser_pilot_beam.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="191" data-file-width="261" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Space_to_ground_microwave%2C_laser_pilot_beam.png/220px-Space_to_ground_microwave%2C_laser_pilot_beam.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/66/Space_to_ground_microwave%2C_laser_pilot_beam.png 1.5x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Space_to_ground_microwave,_laser_pilot_beam.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A laser pilot beam guides the microwave power transmission to a rectenna.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/National_Space_Society" title="National Space Society">
       National Space Society
      </a>
      maintains an extensive
      <b>
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.nss.org/settlement/ssp/library/index.htm" rel="nofollow">
        Space Solar Power Library
       </a>
      </b>
      of all major historical documents and studies associated with space solar power, and
      <a class="external text" href="http://www.nss.org/settlement/ssp/#articles" rel="nofollow">
       major news articles
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1941, science fiction writer
      <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Asimov" title="Isaac Asimov">
       Isaac Asimov
      </a>
      published the science fiction short story "
      <a href="/wiki/Reason_(short_story)" title="Reason (short story)">
       Reason
      </a>
      ", in which a space station transmits energy collected from the Sun to various planets using microwave beams. The SBSP concept, originally known as satellite solar-power system (SSPS), was first described in November 1968.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1973
      <a href="/wiki/Peter_Glaser" title="Peter Glaser">
       Peter Glaser
      </a>
      was granted U.S. patent number 3,781,647 for his method of transmitting power over long distances (e.g. from an SPS to Earth's surface) using
      <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">
       microwaves
      </a>
      from a very large antenna (up to one square kilometer) on the satellite to a much larger one, now known as a
      <a href="/wiki/Rectenna" title="Rectenna">
       rectenna
      </a>
      , on the ground.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glaser_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Glaser-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Glaser then was a vice president at
      <a href="/wiki/Arthur_D._Little" title="Arthur D. Little">
       Arthur D. Little
      </a>
      , Inc. NASA signed a contract with ADL to lead four other companies in a broader study in 1974. They found that, while the concept had several major problems – chiefly the expense of putting the required materials in orbit and the lack of experience on projects of this scale in space – it showed enough promise to merit further investigation and research.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FS_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FS-7">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Between 1978 and 1986, the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">
       Congress
      </a>
      authorized the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">
       Department of Energy
      </a>
      (DoE) and
      <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
       NASA
      </a>
      to jointly investigate the concept. They organized the Satellite Power System Concept Development and Evaluation Program.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The study remains the most extensive performed to date (budget $50 million).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mankins_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mankins-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Several reports were published investigating the engineering feasibility of such an engineering project. They include:
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NASA_solar_power_satellite_concept_1976.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="3020" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/NASA_solar_power_satellite_concept_1976.jpg/220px-NASA_solar_power_satellite_concept_1976.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/NASA_solar_power_satellite_concept_1976.jpg/330px-NASA_solar_power_satellite_concept_1976.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/NASA_solar_power_satellite_concept_1976.jpg/440px-NASA_solar_power_satellite_concept_1976.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NASA_solar_power_satellite_concept_1976.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Artist's concept of Solar Power Satellite in place. Shown is the assembly of a microwave transmission antenna. The solar power satellite was to be located in a geosynchronous orbit, 36,000 miles above the Earth's surface. NASA 1976
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Resource Requirements (Critical Materials, Energy, and Land)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Financial/Management Scenarios
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">

        </a>
       </sup>

      </li>

       State and Local Regulations as Applied to Satellite Power System Microwave Receiving Antenna Facilities

      </li>
      <li>
       Student Participation
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">

      </li>
      <li>
       Potential of Laser for SBSP Power Transmission
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">

        </a>

      </li>
      <li>
       International Agreements
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">

       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">

      <li>

     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Discontinuation">
       Discontinuation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Space-based_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Discontinuation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The project was not continued with the change in administrations after the 1980 US Federal elections. The
      <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Technology_Assessment" title="Office of Technology Assessment">
       Office of Technology Assessment
      </a>
      concluded that "Too little is currently known about the technical, economic, and environmental aspects of SPS to make a sound decision whether to proceed with its development and deployment. In addition, without further research an SPS demonstration or systems-engineering verification program would be a high-risk venture."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1997 NASA conducted its "Fresh Look" study to examine the modern state of SBSP feasibility. In assessing "What has changed" since the DOE study, NASA asserted that the "US National Space Policy now calls for NASA to make significant investments in technology (not a particular vehicle) to drive the costs of ETO
      <i>
       [Earth to Orbit]
      </i>
      transportation down dramatically. This is, of course, an absolute requirement of space solar power."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Conversely, Dr.
      <a href="/wiki/Pete_Worden" title="Pete Worden">
       Pete Worden
      </a>
      claimed that space-based solar is about five orders of magnitude more expensive than solar power from the Arizona desert, with a major cost being the transportation of materials to orbit. Dr. Worden referred to possible solutions as speculative, and that would not be available for decades at the earliest.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_30-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-30">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On Nov 2, 2012, China proposed space collaboration with India that mentioned SBSP, " . . . may be Space-based Solar Power initiative so that both India and China can work for long term association with proper funding along with other willing space faring nations to bring space solar power to earth."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Space_Solar_Power_Exploratory_Research_and_Technology_program">
       Space Solar Power Exploratory Research and Technology program
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Space-based_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Space Solar Power Exploratory Research and Technology program">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-Prose" role="presentation">
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-image">
        <div style="width:52px">
         <img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" width="40"/>
        </div>
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text">
        <span class="mbox-text-span">
         This section
         <b>
          contains
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Embedded_lists" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Embedded lists">
           embedded lists
          </a>
          that may be better presented using
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Prose" title="Wikipedia:Prose">
           prose
          </a>
          .
         </b>
         <span class="hide-when-compact">
          You can help by converting the list or lists to prose, if
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Embedded_lists" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Embedded lists">

          </a>
          .
          <a href="/wiki/Help:Editing" title="Help:Editing">
           Editing help
          </a>
          is available.
         </span>
         <small>
          <i>
           (August 2015)
          </i>
         </small>

         </small>
        </span>

     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Space_Solar_Power_Exploratory_Research_and_Technology_program" title="Space Solar Power Exploratory Research and Technology program">
       Space Solar Power Exploratory Research and Technology program
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar_power_satellite_sandwich_or_abascus_concept.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1593" data-file-width="2048" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Solar_power_satellite_sandwich_or_abascus_concept.jpg/220px-Solar_power_satellite_sandwich_or_abascus_concept.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Solar_power_satellite_sandwich_or_abascus_concept.jpg/330px-Solar_power_satellite_sandwich_or_abascus_concept.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Solar_power_satellite_sandwich_or_abascus_concept.jpg/440px-Solar_power_satellite_sandwich_or_abascus_concept.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solar_power_satellite_sandwich_or_abascus_concept.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        SERT sandwich concept.NASA
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 1999, NASA's Space Solar Power Exploratory Research and Technology program (SERT) was initiated for the following purposes:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Perform design studies of selected flight demonstration concepts.
      </li>
      <li>
       Evaluate studies of the general feasibility, design, and requirements.
      </li>
      <li>
       Create conceptual designs of subsystems that make use of advanced SSP technologies to benefit future space or terrestrial applications.
      </li>
      <li>
       Formulate a preliminary plan of action for the U.S. (working with international partners) to undertake an aggressive technology initiative.
      </li>
      <li>
       Construct technology development and demonstration roadmaps for critical Space Solar Power (SSP) elements.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      SERT went about developing a solar power satellite (SPS) concept for a future
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigawatt" title="Gigawatt">
       gigawatt
      </a>
      space power system, to provide electrical power by converting the Sun's energy and beaming it to Earth's surface, and provided a conceptual development path that would utilize current technologies. SERT proposed an
      <a href="/wiki/Inflatable_space_structures" title="Inflatable space structures">
       inflatable
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photovoltaic" title="Photovoltaic">
       photovoltaic
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Textile_manufacturing_terminology#G" title="Textile manufacturing terminology">
       gossamer
      </a>
      structure with concentrator lenses or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concentrating_solar_power" title="Concentrating solar power">
       solar
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Heat_engine" title="Heat engine">
       heat engines
      </a>
      to convert
      <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">
       sunlight
      </a>
      into electricity. The program looked both at systems in
      <a href="/wiki/Sun-synchronous_orbit" title="Sun-synchronous orbit">
       sun-synchronous orbit
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Geosynchronous_orbit" title="Geosynchronous orbit">
       geosynchronous orbit
      </a>
      . Some of SERT's conclusions:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       The increasing global energy demand is likely to continue for many decades resulting in new power plants of all sizes being built.
      </li>
      <li>
       The environmental impact of those plants and their impact on world energy supplies and geopolitical relationships can be problematic.
      </li>
      <li>
       Renewable energy is a compelling approach, both philosophically and in engineering terms.
      </li>
      <li>
       Many renewable energy sources are limited in their ability to affordably provide the base load power required for global industrial development and prosperity, because of inherent land and water requirements.
      </li>
      <li>
       Based on their Concept Definition Study, space solar power concepts may be ready to reenter the discussion.
      </li>
      <li>
       Solar power satellites should no longer be envisioned as requiring unimaginably large initial investments in fixed infrastructure before the emplacement of productive power plants can begin.
      </li>
      <li>
       Space solar power systems appear to possess many significant environmental advantages when compared to alternative approaches.
      </li>
      <li>
       The economic viability of space solar power systems depends on many factors and the successful development of various new technologies (not least of which is the availability of much lower cost access to space than has been available), however, the same can be said of many other advanced power technologies options.
      </li>
      <li>
       Space solar power may well emerge as a serious candidate among the options for meeting the energy demands of the 21st century. Space Solar Power Satellite Technology Development at the Glenn Research Center—An Overview. James E. Dudenhoefer and Patrick J. George, NASA
       <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Research_Center" title="Glenn Research Center">
        Glenn Research Center
       </a>
       , Cleveland, Ohio.
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_orbital_launch_systems" title="Comparison of orbital launch systems">
        Launch costs
       </a>
       in the range of $100–$200 per kilogram of payload to
       <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">
        low Earth orbit
       </a>
       are needed if SPS are to be economically viable.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mankins_10-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Mankins-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Japan_Aerospace_Exploration_Agency">
       Japan Aerospace Exploration Agency
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Space-based_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Japan Aerospace Exploration Agency">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The May 2014
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IEEE" title="IEEE">
       IEEE
      </a>
      Spectrum magazine carried a lengthy article "It's Always Sunny in Space" by Dr. Susumu Sasaki.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        [32]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The article stated, "It's been the subject of many previous studies and the stuff of sci-fi for decades, but space-based solar power could at last become a reality—and within 25 years, according to a proposal from researchers at the
      <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">
       Tokyo
      </a>
      -based
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japan_Aerospace_Exploration_Agency" title="Japan Aerospace Exploration Agency">
       Japan Aerospace Exploration Agency
      </a>
      (JAXA)."
     </p>
     <p>
      JAXA announced on 12 March 2015 that they wirelessly beamed 1.8 kilowatts 50 meters to a small receiver by converting electricity to microwaves and then back to electricity. This is the standard plan for this type of power.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATarantola_33-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ATarantola-33">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PKT_34-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-PKT-34">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
      On 12 March 2015 Mitsubishi Heavy Industries demonstrated transmission of 10 kilowatts (kW) of power to a receiver unit located at a distance of 500 meters (m) away.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Challenges">
       Challenges
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Space-based_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Challenges">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Potential">
       Potential
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Space-based_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Potential">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The SBSP concept is attractive because space has several major advantages over the Earth's surface for the collection of solar power:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Its always
       <a href="/wiki/Noon#solar_noon" title="Noon">
        solar noon
       </a>
       in space and full sun.
      </li>
      <li>
       Collecting surfaces could receive much more intense sunlight, owing to the lack of obstructions such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_gasses" title="Atmospheric gasses">
        atmospheric gasses
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">
        clouds
       </a>
       , dust and other weather events. Consequently, the intensity in orbit is approximately 144% of the maximum attainable intensity on Earth's surface.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       A satellite could be illuminated over 99% of the time, and be in Earth's
       <a href="/wiki/Shadow" title="Shadow">
        shadow
       </a>
       a maximum of only 72 minutes per night at the spring and fall equinoxes at local midnight.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Orbiting satellites can be exposed to a consistently high degree of solar
       <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">
        radiation
       </a>
       , generally for 24 hours per day, whereas the average earth surface solar panels currently collect power for an average of 29% per day.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_37-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Power could be relatively quickly redirected directly to areas that need it most. A collecting satellite could possibly direct power on demand to different surface locations based on geographical
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baseload" title="Baseload">
        baseload
       </a>
       or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peak_load" title="Peak load">
        peak load
       </a>
       power needs. Typical contracts would be for baseload, continuous power, since peaking power is ephemeral.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Elimination of
       <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">
        plant
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Wildlife" title="Wildlife">
        wildlife
       </a>
       interference.
      </li>
      <li>
       With very large scale implementations, especially at lower altitudes, it potentially can reduce incoming solar radiation reaching earth's surface. This would be desirable for counteracting the effects of
       <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">
        global warming
       </a>
       .
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Drawbacks">
       Drawbacks
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Space-based_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Drawbacks">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The SBSP concept also has a number of problems:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       The large cost of launching a satellite into space
      </li>
      <li>
       Inaccessibility: Maintenance of an earth-based solar panel is relatively simple, but construction and maintenance on a solar panel in space would typically be done telerobotically. In addition to cost, astronauts working in GEO orbit are exposed to unacceptably high radiation dangers and risk and cost about one thousand times more than the same task done telerobotically.
      </li>
      <li>
       The space environment is hostile; panels suffer about 8 times the degradation they would on Earth (except at orbits that are protected by the magnetosphere).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Space_debris" title="Space debris">
        Space debris
       </a>
       is a major hazard to large objects in space, and all large structures such as SBSP systems have been mentioned as potential sources of orbital debris.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       The broadcast frequency of the microwave downlink (if used) would require isolating the SBSP systems away from other satellites. GEO space is already well used and it is considered unlikely the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ITU" title="ITU">
        ITU
       </a>
       would allow an SPS to be launched.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       The large size and corresponding cost of the receiving station on the ground.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Energy losses during several phases of conversion from "photon to electron to photon back to electron," as
       <a href="/wiki/Elon_Musk" title="Elon Musk">
        Elon Musk
       </a>
       has stated.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Design">
       Design
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Space-based_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Design">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solardisk.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1320" data-file-width="2024" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Solardisk.jpg/220px-Solardisk.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Solardisk.jpg/330px-Solardisk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Solardisk.jpg/440px-Solardisk.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solardisk.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Artist's concept of a solar disk on top of a LEO to GEO electrically powered
        <a href="/wiki/Space_tug" title="Space tug">
         space tug
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Space-based solar power essentially consists of three elements:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DOE-SBSP_2-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-DOE-SBSP-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <ol>
      <li>
       collecting solar energy in space with reflectors or inflatable mirrors onto
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">
        solar cells
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wireless_power_transmission" title="Wireless power transmission">
        wireless power transmission
       </a>
       to Earth via
       <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">
        microwave
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">
        laser
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       receiving power on Earth via a
       <a href="/wiki/Rectenna" title="Rectenna">
        rectenna
       </a>
       , a microwave antenna
      </li>
     </ol>
     <p>
      The space-based portion will not need to support itself against gravity (other than relatively weak tidal stresses). It needs no protection from terrestrial wind or weather, but will have to cope with space hazards such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micrometeor" title="Micrometeor">
       micrometeors
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_flares" title="Solar flares">
       solar flares
      </a>
      . Two basic methods of conversion have been studied:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photovoltaic" title="Photovoltaic">
       photovoltaic
      </a>
      (PV) and
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_energy" title="Solar thermal energy">
       solar dynamic
      </a>
      (SD). Most analyses of SBSP have focused on photovoltaic conversion using solar cells that directly convert sunlight into electricity. Solar dynamic uses mirrors to concentrate light on a boiler. The use of solar dynamic could reduce mass per watt.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wireless_power_transmission" title="Wireless power transmission">
       Wireless power transmission
      </a>
      was proposed early on as a means to transfer energy from collection to the Earth's surface, using either microwave or laser radiation at a variety of frequencies.
     </p>
     <p>
      There is an annual
      <a class="external text" href="http://sunsat.gridlab.ohio.edu/" rel="nofollow">
       International SunSat design competition
      </a>
      hosted by Ohio University.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Microwave_power_transmission">
       Microwave power transmission
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Space-based_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Microwave power transmission">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/William_C._Brown" title="William C. Brown">
       William C. Brown
      </a>
      demonstrated in 1964, during
      <a href="/wiki/Walter_Cronkite" title="Walter Cronkite">
       Walter Cronkite
      </a>
      's
      <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">
       CBS
      </a>
      News program, a microwave-powered model
      <a href="/wiki/Helicopter" title="Helicopter">
       helicopter
      </a>
      that received all the power it needed for flight from a microwave beam. Between 1969 and 1975, Bill Brown was technical director of a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JPL" title="JPL">
       JPL
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Raytheon" title="Raytheon">
       Raytheon
      </a>
      program that beamed 30
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt" title="Kilowatt">
       kW
      </a>
      of power over a distance of 1 mile (1.6 km) at 84% efficiency.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_42-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brown-42">
        [42]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Microwave power transmission of tens of kilowatts has been well proven by existing tests at
      <a href="/wiki/Goldstone_Deep_Space_Communications_Complex" title="Goldstone Deep Space Communications Complex">
       Goldstone
      </a>
      in California (1975)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_42-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brown-42">
        [42]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and Grand Bassin on
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reunion_Island" title="Reunion Island">
       Reunion Island
      </a>
      (1997).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comparison_of_Laser_and_Microwave_Power_Transmission_for_Space_Station_Power.gif">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="796" data-file-width="640" height="274" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Comparison_of_Laser_and_Microwave_Power_Transmission_for_Space_Station_Power.gif/220px-Comparison_of_Laser_and_Microwave_Power_Transmission_for_Space_Station_Power.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Comparison_of_Laser_and_Microwave_Power_Transmission_for_Space_Station_Power.gif/330px-Comparison_of_Laser_and_Microwave_Power_Transmission_for_Space_Station_Power.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Comparison_of_Laser_and_Microwave_Power_Transmission_for_Space_Station_Power.gif/440px-Comparison_of_Laser_and_Microwave_Power_Transmission_for_Space_Station_Power.gif 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comparison_of_Laser_and_Microwave_Power_Transmission_for_Space_Station_Power.gif" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Comparison of laser and microwave power transmission. NASA diagram
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      More recently, microwave power transmission has been demonstrated, in conjunction with solar energy capture, between a mountain top in Maui and the island of Hawaii (92 miles away), by a team under John C. Mankins.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Technological challenges in terms of array layout, single radiation element design, and overall efficiency, as well as the associated theoretical limits are presently a subject of research, as it is demonstrated by the Special Session on "Analysis of Electromagnetic Wireless Systems for Solar Power Transmission" to be held in the 2010
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IEEE" title="IEEE">
       IEEE
      </a>
      Symposium on Antennas and Propagation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2013, a useful overview was published, covering technologies and issues associated with microwave power transmission from space to ground. It includes an introduction to SPS, current research and future prospects.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sasaki_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sasaki-49">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Moreover, a review of current methodologies and technologies for the design of antenna arrays for microwave power transmission appeared in the Proceedings of the IEEE
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MASSA_50-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MASSA-50">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Laser_power_beaming">
       Laser power beaming
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Space-based_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Laser power beaming">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">
       Laser
      </a>
      power beaming was envisioned by some at NASA as a stepping stone to further industrialization of space. In the 1980s, researchers at NASA worked on the potential use of lasers for space-to-space power beaming, focusing primarily on the development of a solar-powered laser. In 1989 it was suggested that power could also be usefully beamed by laser from Earth to space. In 1991 the SELENE project (SpacE Laser ENErgy) had begun, which included the study of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laser_power_beaming" title="Laser power beaming">
       laser power beaming
      </a>
      for supplying power to a lunar base. The SELENE program was a two-year research effort, but the cost of taking the concept to operational status was too high, and the official project ended in 1993 before reaching a space-based demonstration.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1988 the use of an Earth-based laser to power an electric thruster for space propulsion was proposed by Grant Logan, with technical details worked out in 1989. He proposed using diamond solar cells operating at 600 degrees to convert
      <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">
       ultraviolet
      </a>
      laser light.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Orbital_location">
       Orbital location
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Space-based_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Orbital location">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The main advantage of locating a space power station in geostationary orbit is that the antenna geometry stays constant, and so keeping the antennas lined up is simpler. Another advantage is that nearly continuous power transmission is immediately available as soon as the first space power station is placed in orbit; other space-based power stations have much longer start-up times before they are producing nearly continuous power. A collection of LEO (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_Earth_Orbit" title="Low Earth Orbit">
       Low Earth Orbit
      </a>
      ) space power stations has been proposed as a precursor to GEO (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geostationary_Orbit" title="Geostationary Orbit">
       Geostationary Orbit
      </a>
      ) space-based solar power.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Earth-based_receiver">
       Earth-based receiver
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Space-based_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Earth-based receiver">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The Earth-based
      <a href="/wiki/Rectenna" title="Rectenna">
       rectenna
      </a>
      would likely consist of many short
      <a href="/wiki/Dipole_antenna" title="Dipole antenna">
       dipole antennas
      </a>
      connected via
      <a href="/wiki/Diode" title="Diode">
       diodes
      </a>
      . Microwave broadcasts from the satellite would be received in the dipoles with about 85% efficiency.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
      With a conventional microwave antenna, the reception efficiency is better, but its cost and complexity are also considerably greater. Rectennas would likely be several kilometers across.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="In_space_applications">
       In space applications
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Space-based_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: In space applications">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      A laser SBSP could also power a base or vehicles on the surface of the Moon or Mars, saving on mass costs to land the power source. A spacecraft or another satellite could also be powered by the same means. In a 2012 report presented to NASA on Space Solar Power, the author mentions another potential use for the technology behind Space Solar Power could be for Solar Electric Propulsion Systems that could be used for interplanetary human exploration missions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        [54]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        [55]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_costs">
       Launch costs
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Space-based_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Launch costs">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      One problem for the SBSP concept is the cost of space launches and the amount of material that would need to be launched.
      <a href="/wiki/Reusable_launch_system" title="Reusable launch system">
       Reusable launch systems
      </a>
      are predicted to provide lower launch costs to low Earth orbit (LEO).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
      As of November 2013
      <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
       <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Space-based_solar_power&amp;action=edit">
        [update]
       </a>
      </sup>
      , one company,
      <a href="/wiki/SpaceX" title="SpaceX">
       SpaceX
      </a>
      , is two years along on a
      <a href="/wiki/Private_spaceflight" title="Private spaceflight">
       privately funded
      </a>
      multi-year
      <a href="/wiki/New_product_development" title="New product development">
       development program
      </a>
      for a
      <a href="/wiki/SpaceX_reusable_launch_system_development_program" title="SpaceX reusable launch system development program">
       reusable rocket launching system
      </a>
      with the stated intention to commercialize "fully and rapidly reusable" launch technology.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pm20120207_59-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pm20120207-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpaceX_Video_from_SpaceX.com_60-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SpaceX_Video_from_SpaceX.com-60">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cspan20110929_61-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cspan20110929-61">
        [61]
       </a>
      </sup>
      SpaceX has completed eight
      <a href="/wiki/Flight_test" title="Flight test">
       test flights
      </a>
      of their low-altitude booster return prototype,
      <a href="/wiki/Grasshopper_(rocket)" title="Grasshopper (rocket)">
       Grasshopper
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn20131017_62-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-sn20131017-62">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and one test flight of a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Falcon_9_ocean_booster_landing_tests" title="Falcon 9 ocean booster landing tests">
       high-altitude/high-velocity booster return test vehicle
      </a>
      , with a second booster return test flight planned for early 2014.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sn20130906_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-sn20130906-63">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-th20131025_64-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-th20131025-64">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Much of the material launched need not be delivered to its eventual orbit immediately, which raises the possibility that high efficiency (but slower) engines could move SPS material from LEO to GEO at an acceptable cost. Examples include
      <a href="/wiki/Ion_thruster" title="Ion thruster">
       ion thrusters
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_propulsion" title="Nuclear propulsion">
       nuclear propulsion
      </a>
      . Power beaming from
      <a href="/wiki/Geostationary_orbit" title="Geostationary orbit">
       geostationary orbit
      </a>
      by microwaves carries the difficulty that the required 'optical aperture' sizes are very large. For example, the 1978 NASA SPS study required a 1-km diameter transmitting antenna, and a 10 km diameter receiving rectenna, for a microwave beam at
      <a href="/wiki/ISM_band" title="ISM band">
       2.45 GHz
      </a>
      . These sizes can be somewhat decreased by using shorter wavelengths, although they have increased
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_absorption" title="Water absorption">
       atmospheric absorption
      </a>
      and even potential beam blockage by rain or water droplets. Because of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thinned_array_curse" title="Thinned array curse">
       thinned array curse
      </a>
      , it is not possible to make a narrower beam by combining the beams of several smaller satellites. The large size of the transmitting and receiving antennas means that the minimum practical power level for an SPS will necessarily be high; small SPS systems will be possible, but uneconomic.
     </p>
     <p>
      To give an idea of the scale of the problem, assuming a solar panel mass of 20 kg per kilowatt (without considering the mass of the supporting structure, antenna, or any significant mass reduction of any focusing mirrors) a 4 GW power station would weigh about 80,000
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_tons" title="Metric tons">
       metric tons
      </a>
      , all of which would, in current circumstances, be launched from the Earth. Very lightweight designs could likely achieve 1 kg/kW,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
      meaning 4,000 metric tons for the solar panels for the same 4 GW capacity station. This would be the equivalent of between 40 and 150
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heavy_Lift_Launch_Vehicle" title="Heavy Lift Launch Vehicle">
       heavy-lift launch vehicle
      </a>
      (HLLV) launches to send the material to low earth orbit, where it would likely be converted into subassembly solar arrays, which then could use high-efficiency ion-engine style rockets to (slowly) reach GEO (
      <a href="/wiki/Geostationary_orbit" title="Geostationary orbit">
       Geostationary orbit
      </a>
      ). With an estimated serial launch cost for shuttle-based HLLVs of $500 million to $800 million, and launch costs for alternative HLLVs at $78 million, total launch costs would range between $11 billion (low cost HLLV, low weight panels) and $320 billion ('expensive' HLLV, heavier panels).
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2010)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      To these costs must be added the environmental impact of heavy space launch emissions, if such costs are to be used in comparison to earth-based energy production. For comparison, the direct cost of a new coal
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
      or nuclear power plant ranges from $3 billion to $6 billion per GW (not including the full cost to the environment from CO2 emissions or storage of spent nuclear fuel, respectively); another example is the
      <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">
       Apollo missions
      </a>
      to the Moon cost a grand total of $24 billion (1970s' dollars), taking inflation into account, would cost $140 billion today, more expensive than the construction of the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">
       International Space Station
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      However, in 2013 based on Recent innovations, Electric Space: Space-Based Solar Power Technologies &amp; Applications suggested a new way to reduce costs by replacing smaller satellites and in lower Orbits.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        [67]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Building_from_space">
       Building from space
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Space-based_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Building from space">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="From_lunar_materials_launched_in_orbit">
       From lunar materials launched in orbit
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Space-based_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: From lunar materials launched in orbit">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gerard_O%27Neill" title="Gerard O'Neill">
       Gerard O'Neill
      </a>
      , noting the problem of high launch costs in the early 1970s, proposed building the SPS's in orbit with materials from the
      <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
       Moon
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        [68]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Colonization_of_the_Moon#Launch_costs" title="Colonization of the Moon">
       Launch costs
      </a>
      from the Moon are potentially much lower than from Earth, due to the lower
      <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">
       gravity
      </a>
      and lack of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_drag" title="Atmospheric drag">
       atmospheric drag
      </a>
      . This 1970s proposal assumed the then-advertised future launch costing of NASA's space shuttle. This approach would require substantial up front capital investment to establish
      <a href="/wiki/Mass_driver" title="Mass driver">
       mass drivers
      </a>
      on the Moon.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        [69]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Nevertheless, on 30 April 1979, the Final Report ("Lunar Resources Utilization for Space Construction") by General Dynamics' Convair Division, under NASA contract NAS9-15560, concluded that use of lunar resources would be cheaper than Earth-based materials for a system of as few as thirty Solar Power Satellites of 10GW capacity each.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        [70]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1980, when it became obvious NASA's launch cost estimates for the space shuttle were grossly optimistic, O'Neill et al. published another route to manufacturing using lunar materials with much lower startup costs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        [71]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This 1980s SPS concept relied less on human presence in space and more on partially
      <a href="/wiki/Self-replicating_machine#Advanced_Automation_for_Space_Missions" title="Self-replicating machine">
       self-replicating systems
      </a>
      on the lunar surface under
      <a href="/wiki/Telepresence" title="Telepresence">
       remote control
      </a>
      of workers stationed on Earth. The high
      <a href="/wiki/Net_energy_gain" title="Net energy gain">
       net energy gain
      </a>
      of this proposal derives from the Moon's much shallower
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_well" title="Gravitational well">
       gravitational well
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Having a relatively cheap per pound source of raw materials from space would lessen the concern for low mass designs and result in a different sort of SPS being built. The low cost per pound of lunar materials in O'Neill's vision would be supported by using lunar material to manufacture more facilities in orbit than just solar power satellites. Advanced techniques for launching from the Moon may reduce the cost of building a solar power satellite from lunar materials. Some proposed techniques include the lunar mass driver and the
      <a href="/wiki/Lunar_space_elevator" title="Lunar space elevator">
       lunar space elevator
      </a>
      , first described by Jerome Pearson.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        [72]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It would require establishing
      <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">
       silicon
      </a>
      mining and solar cell manufacturing facilities on the
      <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
       Moon
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2010)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="On_the_Moon">
       On the Moon
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Space-based_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: On the Moon">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/David_Criswell" title="David Criswell">
       David Criswell
      </a>
      suggests the Moon is the optimum location for solar power stations, and promotes
      <b>
       lunar solar power
      </b>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        [73]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        [74]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The main advantage he envisions is construction largely from locally available lunar materials, using
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/In-situ_resource_utilization" title="In-situ resource utilization">
       in-situ resource utilization
      </a>
      , with a
      <a href="/wiki/Remote_manipulator" title="Remote manipulator">
       teleoperated
      </a>
      mobile factory and crane to assemble the microwave reflectors, and rovers to assemble and pave solar cells,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        [75]
       </a>
      </sup>
      which would significantly reduce launch costs compared to SBSP designs. Power relay satellites orbiting around earth and the Moon reflecting the microwave beam are also part of the project. A demo project of 1 GW starts at $50 billion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        [76]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Shimizu_Corporation" title="Shimizu Corporation">
       Shimizu Corporation
      </a>
      use combination of lasers and microwave for the lunar ring concept, along with power relay satellites.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        [77]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        [78]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="From_an_asteroid">
       From an asteroid
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Space-based_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: From an asteroid">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_mining" title="Asteroid mining">
       Asteroid mining
      </a>
      has also been seriously considered. A NASA design study
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
      evaluated a 10,000 ton mining vehicle (to be assembled in orbit) that would return a 500,000 ton asteroid fragment to geostationary orbit. Only about 3,000 tons of the mining ship would be traditional aerospace-grade payload. The rest would be reaction mass for the mass-driver engine, which could be arranged to be the spent rocket stages used to launch the payload. Assuming that 100% of the returned asteroid was useful, and that the asteroid miner itself couldn't be reused, that represents nearly a 95% reduction in launch costs. However, the true merits of such a method would depend on a thorough mineral survey of the candidate asteroids; thus far, we have only estimates of their composition.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
       <a href="#cite_note-80">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
      One proposal is to capture the asteroid
      <a href="/wiki/99942_Apophis" title="99942 Apophis">
       Apophis
      </a>
      into earth orbit and convert it into 150 solar power satellites of 5 GW each or the larger asteroid 1999 AN10 which is 50x the size of Apophis and large enough to build 7,500 5-Gigawatt Solar Power Satellites
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Counter_arguments">
       Counter arguments
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Space-based_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Counter arguments">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">
       Safety
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Space-based_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Safety">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The use of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microwave_power_transmission" title="Microwave power transmission">
       microwave transmission of power
      </a>
      has been the most controversial issue in considering any SPS design. At the Earth's surface, a suggested microwave beam would have a maximum intensity at its center, of 23 mW/cm
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      (less than 1/4 the
      <a href="/wiki/Sunlight#Intensity_in_the_Solar_System" title="Sunlight">
       solar irradiation constant
      </a>
      ), and an intensity of less than 1 mW/cm
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      outside the rectenna fenceline (the receiver's perimeter).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intensity_82-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-intensity-82">
        [82]
       </a>
      </sup>
      These compare with current United States
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Act" title="Occupational Safety and Health Act">
       Occupational Safety and Health Act
      </a>
      (OSHA) workplace exposure limits for microwaves, which are 10 mW/cm
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        [83]
       </a>
      </sup>
      - the limit itself being expressed in voluntary terms and ruled unenforceable for Federal OSHA enforcement purposes.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2009)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      A beam of this intensity is therefore at its center, of a similar magnitude to current safe workplace levels, even for long term or indefinite exposure. Outside the receiver, it is far less than the OSHA long-term levels
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
       <a href="#cite_note-84">
        [84]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Over 95% of the beam energy will fall on the rectenna. The remaining microwave energy will be absorbed and dispersed well within standards currently imposed upon microwave emissions around the world.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is important for system efficiency that as much of the microwave radiation as possible be focused on the rectenna. Outside the rectenna, microwave intensities rapidly decrease, so nearby towns or other human activity should be completely unaffected.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEEE_602864_86-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-IEEE_602864-86">
        [86]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Exposure to the beam is able to be minimized in other ways. On the ground, physical access is controllable (e.g., via fencing), and typical aircraft flying through the beam provide passengers with a protective metal shell (i.e., a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faraday_Cage" title="Faraday Cage">
       Faraday Cage
      </a>
      ), which will intercept the microwaves. Other aircraft (
      <a href="/wiki/Balloon" title="Balloon">
       balloons
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultralight" title="Ultralight">
       ultralight
      </a>
      , etc.) can avoid exposure by observing airflight control spaces, as is currently done for military and other controlled airspace. The microwave beam intensity at ground level in the center of the beam would be designed and physically built into the system; simply, the transmitter would be too far away and too small to be able to increase the intensity to unsafe levels, even in principle.
     </p>
     <p>
      In addition, a design constraint is that the microwave beam must not be so intense as to injure wildlife, particularly birds. Experiments with deliberate microwave irradiation at reasonable levels have failed to show negative effects even over multiple generations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        [87]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Suggestions have been made to locate rectennas offshore,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        [88]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        [89]
       </a>
      </sup>
      but this presents serious problems, including corrosion, mechanical stresses, and biological contamination.
     </p>
     <p>
      A commonly proposed approach to ensuring fail-safe beam targeting is to use a retrodirective
      <a href="/wiki/Phased_array" title="Phased array">
       phased array
      </a>
      antenna/rectenna. A "pilot" microwave beam emitted from the center of the rectenna on the ground establishes a phase front at the transmitting antenna. There, circuits in each of the antenna's subarrays compare the pilot beam's phase front with an internal clock phase to control the phase of the outgoing signal. This forces the transmitted beam to be centered precisely on the rectenna and to have a high degree of phase uniformity; if the pilot beam is lost for any reason (if the transmitting antenna is turned away from the rectenna, for example) the phase control value fails and the microwave power beam is automatically defocused.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEEE_602864_86-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-IEEE_602864-86">
        [86]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Such a system would be physically incapable of focusing its power beam anywhere that did not have a pilot beam transmitter. The long-term effects of beaming power through the ionosphere in the form of microwaves has yet to be studied, but nothing has been suggested which might lead to any significant effect.
     </p>

      <span class="mw-headline" id="Timeline">

     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_20th_century">
       In the 20th century
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Space-based_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: In the 20th century">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <b>
        1941
       </b>
       : Isaac Asimov published the science fiction short story "Reason," in which a space station transmits energy collected from the sun to various planets using microwave beams.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        1968
       </b>
       : Dr.
       <a href="/wiki/Peter_Glaser" title="Peter Glaser">
        Peter Glaser
       </a>
       introduces the concept of a "solar power satellite" system with square miles of solar collectors in high
       <a href="/wiki/Geosynchronous_orbit" title="Geosynchronous orbit">
        geosynchronous orbit
       </a>
       for collection and conversion of sun's energy into a
       <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">
        microwave
       </a>
       beam to transmit usable energy to large receiving antennas (
       <a href="/wiki/Rectenna" title="Rectenna">
        rectennas
       </a>
       ) on Earth for distribution.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        1973
       </b>
       : Dr. Peter Glaser is granted
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_patent" title="United States patent">
        United States patent
       </a>
       number 3,781,647 for his method of transmitting power over long distances using microwaves from a large (one square kilometer) antenna on the satellite to a much larger one on the ground, now known as a rectenna.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glaser_6-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Glaser-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        1978–81
       </b>
       : The
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">
        United States Department of Energy
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
        NASA
       </a>
       examine the solar power satellite (SPS) concept extensively, publishing design and feasibility studies.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        1982
       </b>
       :
       <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">
        Boeing
       </a>
       proposal
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">

         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>

       <b>

       </b>

       <a href="/wiki/Stationary_High_Altitude_Relay_Platform" title="Stationary High Altitude Relay Platform">
        Stationary High Altitude Relay Platform
       </a>
       a Canadian experiment
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        1994
       </b>
       : The
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">

       </a>

       <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Photovoltaic_Experiment" title="Advanced Photovoltaic Experiment">
        Advanced Photovoltaic Experiment
       </a>

       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pegasus_rocket" title="Pegasus rocket">
        Pegasus rocket
       </a>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        1995–97
       </b>
       : NASA conducts a "Fresh Look" study of space solar power (SSP) concepts and technologies.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        1998
       </b>
       : The Space Solar Power Concept Definition Study (CDS) identifies credible, commercially viable SSP concepts, while pointing out technical and programmatic risks.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        1998
       </b>

      </li>

       <b>
        1999
       </b>

       <a href="/wiki/Space_Solar_Power_Exploratory_Research_and_Technology_program" title="Space Solar Power Exploratory Research and Technology program">

       </a>
       (
       <i>
        <a href="#SERT">
         SERT, see below
        </a>
       </i>

      </li>
      <li>

       </b>

       <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">
        U.S. House of Representatives
       </a>
       , saying "Large-scale SSP is a very complex integrated system of systems that requires numerous significant advances in current technology and capabilities. A technology roadmap has been developed that lays out potential paths for achieving all needed advances — albeit over several decades.

There is a risk element but you can reduce it... You can put these small receivers in the desert or in the mountains away from populated areas. ...We believe that in 15 to 25 years we can lower that cost to 7 to 10 cents per kilowatt hour. ...We offer an advantage.
