     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Non-typical_configurations_and_architectural_considerations">
       Non-typical configurations and architectural considerations
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Space-based_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Non-typical configurations and architectural considerations">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The typical reference system-of-systems involves a significant number (several thousand multi-gigawatt systems to service all or a significant portion of Earth's energy requirements) of individual satellites in GEO. The typical reference design for the individual satellite is in the 1-10 GW range and usually involves planar or concentrated solar photovoltics (PV) as the energy collector / conversion. The most typical transmission designs are in the 1–10 GHz (2.45 or 5.8 GHz) RF band where there are minimum losses in the atmosphere. Materials for the satellites are sourced from, and manufactured on Earth and expected to be transported to LEO via re-usable rocket launch, and transported between LEO and GEO via chemical or electrical propulsion. In summary, the architecture choices are:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <b>
        Location = GEO
       </b>
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Energy Collection = PV
       </b>
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Satellite = Monolithic Structure
       </b>
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Transmission = RF
       </b>
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Materials &amp; Manufacturing = Earth
       </b>
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Installation = RLVs to LEO, Chemical to GEO
       </b>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      There are several interesting design variants from the reference system:
     </p>
     <p>
      <b>
       Alternate energy collection location
      </b>
      : While GEO is most typical because of its advantages of nearness to Earth, simplified pointing and tracking, very small time in occultation, and scalability to meet all global demand several times over, other locations have been proposed:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <b>
        Sun Earth L1:
       </b>
       Robert Kennedy III, Ken Roy &amp; David Fields have proposed a variant of the L1 sunshade called "
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0094576512004006" rel="nofollow">
        Dyson Dot
       </a>
       s" where a multi-terrawatt primary collector would beam energy back to a series of LEO sun-synchronous receiver satellites. The much farther distance to Earth requires a correspondingly larger transmission aperture.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Lunar Surface
       </b>
       : Dr.
       <a href="/wiki/David_Criswell" title="David Criswell">
        David Criswell
       </a>
       has
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.lunarsolarpower.org/#!criswell/czxo" rel="nofollow">
        proposed using the Lunar surface
       </a>
       itself as the collection medium, beaming power to the ground via a series of microwave reflectors in Earth Orbit. The chief advantage of this approach would be the ability to manufacture the solar collectors in-situ without the energy cost and complexity of launch. Disadvantages include the much longer distance, requiring larger transmission systems, the required "overbuild" to deal with the lunar night, and the difficulty of sufficient manufacturing and pointing of reflector satellites.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        MEO:
       </b>
       MEO systems have been proposed for in-space utilities and beam-power propulsion infrastructures. For example, see Royce Jones'
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.earthspaceagency.org/images/stories/BeamedEnergyPublish.pdf" rel="nofollow">
        paper
       </a>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Highly Elliptical Orbits:
       </b>
       Molniya, Tundra, or Quazi Zenith orbits have been proposed as early locations for niche markets, requiring less energy to access and providing good persistence. For example, see
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.sspi.gatech.edu/welsom_isdc_reed.pdf" rel="nofollow">
        Kevin Reed's QGSO proposal (Slide 25)
       </a>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Sun-Sync LEO:
       </b>
       In this near Polar Orbit, the satellites precess at a rate that allows them to always face the Sun as they rotate around Earth. This is an easy to access orbit requiring far less energy, and its proximity to Earth requires smaller (and therefore less massive) transmitting apertures. However disadvantages to this approach include having to constantly shift receiving stations, or storing energy for a burst transmission. This orbit is already crowded and has significant space debris.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Equatorial LEO:
       </b>
       Japan's
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.spacefuture.com/power/sps2000.shtml" rel="nofollow">
        SPS 2000
       </a>
       proposed an early demonstrator in equatorial LEO in which multiple equatorial participating nations could receive some power.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Earth's Surface
       </b>
       : Dr. Narayan Komerath has proposed a
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.adl.gatech.edu/archives/adlp06100501.pdf" rel="nofollow">
        space power grid
       </a>
       where excess energy from an existing grid or power plant on one side of the planet can be passed up to orbit, across to another satellite and down to receivers.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      <b>
       Energy Collection:
      </b>
      The most typical designs for Solar Power Satellites include photovoltaics. These may be planar (and usually passively cooled), concentrated (and perhaps actively cooled). However, there are multiple interesting variants.
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <b>
        Solar Thermal:
       </b>
       Proponents of Solar Thermal have proposed using concentrated heating to cause a state change in a fluid to extract energy via rotating machinery followed by cooling in radiators. Advantages of this method might include overall system mass (disputed), non-degradation due to solar-wind damage, and radiation tolerance. One recent thermal solar power satellite design by Keith Henson has been visualized here.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Solar Pumped Laser:
       </b>
       Japan has pursued a solar-pumped laser, where sunlight directly excites the lasing medium used to create the coherent beam to Earth.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Fusion Decay:
       </b>
       This version of a power-satellite is not "solar". Rather, the vacuum of space is seen as a "feature not a bug" for traditional fusion. Per Dr. Paul Werbos, after fusion even neutral particles decay to charged particles which in a sufficiently large volume would allow direct conversion to current.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        <a href="/wiki/Dyson%E2%80%93Harrop_satellite" title="Dyson–Harrop satellite">
         Solar Wind Loop
        </a>
        :
       </b>
       Also called a
       <a href="/wiki/Dyson%E2%80%93Harrop_satellite" title="Dyson–Harrop satellite">
        Dyson-Haroo
       </a>
       p satellite. Here the satellite makes use not of the photons from the Sun but rather the charged particles in the solar wind which via electro-magnetic coupling generate a current in a large loop.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Direct Mirrors:
       </b>
       Early concepts for direct mirror re-direction of light to planet Earth suffered from the problem that rays coming from the sun are not parallel but are expanding from a disk and so the size of the spot on the Earth is quite large. Dr. Lewis Fraas has explored an
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.mtu.edu/ece/department/faculty/full-time/zekavat/SSP2015_fraas.pdf" rel="nofollow">
        array of parbolic mirrors
       </a>
       to augment existing solar arrays.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      <b>
       Alternate Satellite Architecture:
      </b>
      The typical satellite is a monolithic structure composed of a structural truss, one or more collectors, one or more transmitters, and occasionally primary and secondary reflectors. The entire structure may be gravity gradient stabilized. Alternative designs include:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <b>
        Swarms of Smaller Satellites
       </b>
       : Some designs propose swarms of free-flying smaller satellites. This is the case with several laser designs, and appears to be the case with CALTECH's
       <a class="external text" href="http://climate.nasa.gov/news/2426/" rel="nofollow">
        Flying Carpets
       </a>
       . For RF designs, an engineering constraint is the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sparse_arrray_curse" title="Sparse arrray curse">
        sparse array
       </a>
       problem.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Free Floating Components
       </b>
       :
       <a href="/wiki/Solaren" title="Solaren">
        Solaren
       </a>
       has proposed an
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.bldgblog.com/2009/04/space-based-storm-control/" rel="nofollow">
        alternative
       </a>
       to the monolithic structure where the primary reflector and transmission reflector are free-flying.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Spin Stabilization:
       </b>
       NASA explored a spin-stabilized thin film concept.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        <a href="/wiki/Photonic_laser_thruster" title="Photonic laser thruster">
         Photonic Laser Thruster
        </a>
        (PLT) stabilized structure:
       </b>
       Dr. Young Bae has proposed that photon pressure may substitute for compressive members in large structures.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      <b>
       Transmission:
      </b>
      The most typical design for energy transmission is via an RF antenna at below 10 GHz to a rectenna on the ground. Controversy exists between the benefits of Klystrons, Gyrotrons, Magnetrons and solid state. Alternate transmission approaches include:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <b>
        Laser:
       </b>
       Lasers offer the advantage of much lower cost and mass to first power, however there is controversy regarding benefits of efficiency. Lasers allow for much smaller transmitting and receiving apertures. However, a highly concentrated beam has eye-safety, fire safety, and weaponization concerns. Proponents believe they have answers to all these concerns. A laser-based approach must also find alternate ways of coping with precipitation.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Atmospheric Waveguide:
       </b>
       Some have proposed it may be possible to use a short pulse laser to
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Www.dtic.mil/cgi-bin/GetTRDoc%3FAD%3DADA500916&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Www.dtic.mil/cgi-bin/GetTRDoc?AD=ADA500916 (page does not exist)">
        create an atmospheric waveguide
       </a>
       through which concentrated microwaves could flow.
       <a class="external autonumber" href="http://physics.aps.org/articles/v7/21" rel="nofollow">
        [3]
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
        <a href="#cite_note-116">
         [116]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Scalar:
       </b>
       Some have even speculated it may be possible to
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.k-meyl.de/go/Primaerliteratur/PIERS_Proceedings_WPT.pdf" rel="nofollow">
        transmit power through scalar waves
       </a>
       .
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      <b>
       Materials and Manufacturing:
      </b>
      Typical designs make use of the developed industrial manufacturing system extant on Earth, and use Earth based materials both for the satellite and propellant. Variants include:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <b>
        Lunar Materials:
       </b>
       Designs exist for Solar Power Satellites that source &gt;99% of materials from lunar regolith with very small inputs of "vitamins" from other locations. Using materials from the Moon is attractive because launch from the Moon is in theory far less complicated than from Earth. There is no atmosphere, and so components do not need to be packed tightly in an aeroshell and survive vibration, pressure and temperature loads. Launch may be via a magnetic mass driver and the requirement to use propellant for launch entirely. Launch from the Moon the GEO also requires far less energy than from Earth's much deeper gravity well. Building all the solar power satellites to fully supply all the required energy for the entire planet requires less than one millionth of the mass of the Moon.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Self-Replication on the Moon:
       </b>
       NASA explored a
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.nss.org/settlement/moon/library/1982-SelfReplicatingLunarFactory.pdf" rel="nofollow">
        self-replicating factory
       </a>
       on the Moon in 1980. More recently, Justin Lewis-Webber
       <a class="external text" href="http://online.liebertpub.com/doi/pdf/10.1089/space.2015.0041" rel="nofollow">
        proposed
       </a>
       a method of speciated manufacture of core elements based upon
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.artemisinnovation.com/aboutus.html" rel="nofollow">
        John Mankins
       </a>
       <a class="external text" href="https://www.nasa.gov/pdf/716070main_Mankins_2011_PhI_SPS_Alpha.pdf" rel="nofollow">
        SPS-Alpha
       </a>
       design.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Asteroidal Materials:
       </b>
       Some asteroids are thought to have even lower Delta-V to recover materials than the Moon, and some particular materials of interest such as metals may be more concentrated or easier to access.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        In-Space/In-Situ Manufacturing:
       </b>
       With the advent of in-space additive manufacturing, concepts such as
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.tethers.com/SpiderFab.html" rel="nofollow">
        SpiderFab
       </a>
       might allow mass launch of raw materials for local extrusion.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      <b>
       Method of Installation / Transportation of Material to Energy Collection Location
      </b>
      : In the reference designs, component material is launched via well-understood chemical rockets (usually fully reusable launch systems) to LEO, after which either chemical or electrical propulsion is used to carry them to GEO. The desired characteristics for this system is very high mass-flow at low total cost. Alternate concepts include:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <b>
        Lunar Chemical Launch
       </b>
       : ULA has recently
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.ulalaunch.com/uploads/docs/Published_Papers/Commercial_Space/SSP_12_15_sowers.pdf" rel="nofollow">
        showcased a concept
       </a>
       for a fully re-usable chemical lander
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.ulalaunch.com/uploads/docs/Published_Papers/Commercial_Space/SSP_12_15_sowers.pdf" rel="nofollow">
        XEUS
       </a>
       to move materials from the Lunar surface to LLO or GEO.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Lunar
        <a href="/wiki/Mass_driver" title="Mass driver">
         Mass Driver
        </a>
        :
       </b>
       Launch of materials from the lunar surface using a system similar to an aircraft carrier electromagnetic catapult. An unexplored compact alternative would be the slingatron.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        <a href="/wiki/Lunar_space_elevator" title="Lunar space elevator">
         Lunar Space Elevator
        </a>
        :
       </b>
       An equatorial or near-equatorial cable extends to and through the lagrange point. This is claimed by proponents to be lower in mass than a traditional mass driver.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        <a href="/wiki/Space_elevator" title="Space elevator">
         Space Elevator
        </a>
       </b>
       : A ribbon of pure carbon nanotubes extends from its center of gravity in Geostationary orbit, allowing climbers to climb up to GEO. Problems with this include the material challenge of creating a ribbon of such length with adequate strength, management of collisions with satellites and space debris, and lightning.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        MEO Skyhook:
       </b>
       As part of an AFRL study, Roger Lenard proposed a MEO Skyhook. It appears that a gravity gradient-stabilized tether with its center of mass in MEO can be constructed of available materials. The bottom of the skyhook is close to the atmosphere in a "non-keplerian orbit". A re-usable rocket can launch to match altitude and speed with the bottom of the tether which is in a non-keplerian orbit (travelling much slower than typical orbital speed). The payload is transferred and it climbs the cable. The cable itself is kept from de-orbiting via electric propusion and/or electromagnetic effects.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        <a href="/wiki/Maglev" title="Maglev">
         MAGLEV
        </a>
        Launch /
        <a href="/wiki/StarTram" title="StarTram">
         StarTram
        </a>
        :
       </b>
       John Powell has a concept for a
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.startram.com/" rel="nofollow">
        very high mass-flow system
       </a>
       . In a first-gen system, built into a mountain, accelerates a payload through an evacuated MAGLEV track. A small on-board rocket circulizes the payload.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Beamed Energy Launch:
       </b>
       <a href="/wiki/Kevin_L.G._Parkin" title="Kevin L.G. Parkin">
        Kevin Parkin
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Escape_Dynamics" title="Escape Dynamics">
        Escape Dynamics
       </a>
       both have
       <a class="external text" href="http://thesis.library.caltech.edu/2405/" rel="nofollow">
        concepts
       </a>
       for ground-based irradiation of a mono-propellant launch vehicle using RF energy. The RF energy is absorbed and directly heats the propellant not unlike in NERVA-style nuclear-thermal. LaserMotive has a concept for a laser-based approach.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="In_fiction">
       In fiction
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Space-based_solar_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: In fiction">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Space stations transmitting solar power have appeared in science-fiction works like
      <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Asimov" title="Isaac Asimov">
       Isaac Asimov
      </a>
      's "
      <a href="/wiki/Reason_(short_story)" title="Reason (short story)">
       Reason
      </a>
      " (1941), that centers around the troubles caused by
      <a href="/wiki/Robot_series_(Asimov)" title="Robot series (Asimov)">
       the robots
      </a>
      operating the station. Asimov's short story "
      <a href="/wiki/The_Last_Question" title="The Last Question">
       The Last Question
      </a>
      " also features the use of SBSP to provide limitless energy for use on Earth.
      <a href="/wiki/Ben_Bova" title="Ben Bova">
       Ben Bova
      </a>
      's thriller
      <a class="external text" href="http://www.amazon.com/Powersat-Grand-Tour-Ben-Bova/dp/0765348179" rel="nofollow">
       PowerSat
      </a>
      involves a billionaire bent on creating a solar powersat while others try to sabotage it.
     </p>
     <p>
      In the video game
      <a href="/wiki/Sid_Meier%27s_Alpha_Centauri" title="Sid Meier's Alpha Centauri">
       Sid Meier's Alpha Centauri
      </a>
      , the player can construct a city improvement called an "Orbital Power Transmitter" which, while expensive, provides energy to all other cities. Constructing many of these results in huge bonuses to energy production for all cities the player owns. In the novel "Skyfall" (1976) by
      <a href="/wiki/Harry_Harrison_(writer)" title="Harry Harrison (writer)">
       Harry Harrison
      </a>
      an attempt to launch the core of powersat from Cape Canaveral ends in disaster when the launch vehicle fails trapping the payload in a decaying orbit. Several
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simcity" title="Simcity">
       Simcity
      </a>
      games have featured space-microwave power plants as buildable options for municipal energy, along with (unrealistic) disaster scenarios where the beam strays off the collector and sets fire to nearby areas. In the manga and anime
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Mobile_Suit_Gundam_00" title="Mobile Suit Gundam 00">
        Mobile Suit Gundam 00
       </a>
      </i>
      , an orbital ring containing multiple solar collectors and microwave transmitters, along with power stations and space elevators for carrying power back down to Earth's surface, are the primary source of electricity for the Earth in the 24th century.
     </p>
     <p>
      Various aerospace companies have also showcased imaginative future solar power satellites in their corporate vision videos, including the Boeing
      <a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=nEjPLHmFAM8" rel="nofollow">
       You Just Wait
      </a>
      , Lockheed Martin's
      <a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=NQxfJzl2jkg" rel="nofollow">
       The Next 100 Years
      </a>
      , and United Launch Alliance
      <a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=uxftPmpt7aA" rel="nofollow">
       CIS-Lunar 1000
      </a>
      .
     </p>

           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>

         <a class="external text" href="http://www.solarstorms.org/Svulnerability.html" rel="nofollow">
          "Solar Panel Degradation"
         </a>
         whereas on Earth, commercial panels degrade at a rate around 0.25% a year.
         <a class="external text" href="http://www.greenbuildingadvisor.com/blogs/dept/musings/testing-thirty-year-old-photovoltaic-module" rel="nofollow">

       <li id="cite_note-70">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-70">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <cite class="citation book">
          General Dynamics Convair Division (1979).
          <a class="external text" href="http://ntrs.nasa.gov/archive/nasa/casi.ntrs.nasa.gov/19830077470_1983077470.pdf" rel="nofollow">
           <i>
            Lunar Resources Utilization for Space Construction
           </i>
          </a>
          <span style="font-size:85%;">
           (PDF)
          </span>
          . GDC-ASP79-001.
         </cite>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASpace-based+solar+power&amp;rft.au=General+Dynamics+Convair+Division&amp;rft.btitle=Lunar+Resources+Utilization+for+Space+Construction&amp;rft.date=1979&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fntrs.nasa.gov%2Farchive%2Fnasa%2Fcasi.ntrs.nasa.gov%2F19830077470_1983077470.pdf&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-71">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-71">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <cite class="citation journal">
          <a href="/wiki/Gerard_K._O%27Neill" title="Gerard K. O'Neill">
           O'Neill, Gerard K.
          </a>
          ; Driggers, G.;
          <a href="/wiki/Brian_O%27Leary" title="Brian O'Leary">
           O'Leary, B.
          </a>
          (1980). "New Routes to Manufacturing in Space".
          <i>
           Astronautics and Aeronautics
          </i>
          .
          <b>
           18
          </b>
          : 46–51.
         </cite>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASpace-based+solar+power&amp;rft.atitle=New+Routes+to+Manufacturing+in+Space&amp;rft.au=Driggers%2C+G.&amp;rft.aufirst=Gerard+K.&amp;rft.aulast=O%27Neill&amp;rft.au=O%27Leary%2C+B.&amp;rft.date=1980&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Astronautics+and+Aeronautics&amp;rft.pages=46-51&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=18">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
         Several scenarios for the buildup of industry in space are described. One scenario involves a manufacturing facility, manned by a crew of three, entirely on the lunar surface. Another scenario involves a fully automated manufacturing facility, remotely supervised from the earth, with provision for occasional visits by repair crews. A third case involves a manned facility on the Moon for operating a mass-driver launcher to transport lunar materials to a collection point in space and for replicating mass-drivers.
        </span>
       </li>

      <span class="mw-headline" id="External_links">

      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>

     <ul>
      <li>
       <a class="external autonumber" href="http://www.esa.int/gsp/ACT/nrg/projects/SPS.html" rel="nofollow">
        [4]
       </a>
       ESA–Advanced concepts team, Space-based solar power
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://seedmagazine.com/content/article/getting_solar_off_the_ground/" rel="nofollow">
        William Maness on why alternative energy and power grids aren't good playmates and his plans for beaming solar power from space.
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Seed_(magazine)" title="Seed (magazine)">
        Seed (magazine)
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.space.com/opinionscolumns/opinions/glaser_000223.html" rel="nofollow">
        The World Needs Energy from Space
       </a>
       Space-based solar technology is the key to the world's energy and environmental future, writes
       <a href="/wiki/Peter_Glaser" title="Peter Glaser">
        Peter E. Glaser
       </a>
       , a pioneer of the technology.
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://gltrs.grc.nasa.gov/cgi-bin/GLTRS/browse.pl?2004/TM-2004-212743.html" rel="nofollow">

       </a>
       , NASA 2004-212743, report by
       <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_A._Landis" title="Geoffrey A. Landis">
        Geoffrey A. Landis
       </a>
       of NASA Glenn Research Center
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.spacedaily.com/news/ssp-01a.html" rel="nofollow">
        Japan's plans for a Solar Power Station in Space
       </a>
       - the Japanese government hopes to assemble a space-based solar array by 2040.
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.spaceenergy.com/" rel="nofollow">
        Space Energy, Inc.
       </a>

      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.thespacereview.com/article/214/1" rel="nofollow">
        Whatever happened to solar power satellites?
       </a>
       An article that covers the hurdles in the way of deploying a solar power satellite.
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://permanent.com/p-sps.htm" rel="nofollow">
        Solar Power Satellite from Lunar and Asteroidal Materials
       </a>
       Provides an overview of the technological and political developments needed to construct and utilize a multi-gigawatt power satellite. Also provides some perspective on the cost savings achieved by using
       <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_materials" title="Extraterrestrial materials">
        extraterrestrial materials
       </a>
       in the construction of the satellite.
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.thespacereview.com/article/931/1" rel="nofollow">
        A renaissance for space solar power? by Jeff Foust, Monday, August 13, 2007
       </a>
       Reports on renewed institutional interest in SSP, and a lack of such interest in past decades.
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.spacefuture.com/archive/conceptual_study_of_a_solar_power_satellite_sps_2000.shtml" rel="nofollow">

       2010 IEEE International Symposium on Antennas and Propagation

      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.festivaldellecittaimpresa.it/index.php?option=com_events&amp;Itemid=28&amp;lang=en" rel="nofollow">
